  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_0f9be3da-9730-4108-aecb-fc38aad6be1d'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  <div id='choiceBadge'>

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer6584936074727669131">From the award-winning, <em>New York Times</em> bestselling author of <em>NOS4A2</em> and <em>Heart-Shaped Box</em> comes a chilling novel about a worldwide pandemic of spontaneous combustion that threatens to reduce civilization to ashes and a band of improbable heroes who battle to save it, led by one powerful and enigmatic man known as the Fireman.<br/><br/><em>The fireman is coming. Stay cool.</em><br/><br/>No one knows exa</span>
  <span id="freeText6584936074727669131" style="display:none">From the award-winning, <em>New York Times</em> bestselling author of <em>NOS4A2</em> and <em>Heart-Shaped Box</em> comes a chilling novel about a worldwide pandemic of spontaneous combustion that threatens to reduce civilization to ashes and a band of improbable heroes who battle to save it, led by one powerful and enigmatic man known as the Fireman.<br /><br /><em>The fireman is coming. Stay cool.</em><br /><br />No one knows exactly when it began or where it originated. A terrifying new plague is spreading like wildfire across the country, striking cities one by one: Boston, Detroit, Seattle. The doctors call it Draco Incendia Trychophyton. To everyone else it’s Dragonscale, a highly contagious, deadly spore that marks its hosts with beautiful black and gold marks across their bodies—before causing them to burst into flames. Millions are infected; blazes erupt everywhere. There is no antidote. No one is safe.<br /><br />Harper Grayson, a compassionate, dedicated nurse as pragmatic as Mary Poppins, treated hundreds of infected patients before her hospital burned to the ground. Now she’s discovered the telltale gold-flecked marks on her skin. When the outbreak first began, she and her husband, Jakob, had made a pact: they would take matters into their own hands if they became infected. To Jakob’s dismay, Harper wants to live—at least until the fetus she is carrying comes to term. At the hospital, she witnessed infected mothers give birth to healthy babies and believes hers will be fine too. . . if she can live long enough to deliver the child.<br /><br />Convinced that his do-gooding wife has made him sick, Jakob becomes unhinged, and eventually abandons her as their placid New England community collapses in terror. The chaos gives rise to ruthless Cremation Squads—armed, self-appointed posses roaming the streets and woods to exterminate those who they believe carry the spore. But Harper isn’t as alone as she fears: a mysterious and compelling stranger she briefly met at the hospital, a man in a dirty yellow fire fighter’s jacket, carrying a hooked iron bar, straddles the abyss between insanity and death. Known as The Fireman, he strolls the ruins of New Hampshire, a madman afflicted with Dragonscale who has learned to control the fire within himself, using it as a shield to protect the hunted . . . and as a weapon to avenge the wronged.<br /><br />In the desperate season to come, as the world burns out of control, Harper must learn the Fireman’s secrets before her life—and that of her unborn child—goes up in smoke.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6584936074727669131" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/779012-i-am-more-than-a-third-of-the-way-through">I am more than a third of the way through the book and I AM SO BORED. Is anyone else finding this book a snoozefest? I read and loved NOS4A2 (exciting and terrifying) and am not finding an ounce of that same sense of fun in this story.</a>
</div>

<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_779012'><a id="like_count_community_question_779012" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/779012?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">93 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F29875363-the-fireman">like</a></span>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/39251100-abby"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1421464499i/39251100._UY100_CR30,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/39251100-abby">Abby</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I loved NOS4R2, and I was really looking forward to this one... but good God was this tough to get through. Like, REALLY tough. <br/><br/>I thought the first 10<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I loved NOS4R2, and I was really looking forward to this one... but good God was this tough to get through. Like, REALLY tough. <br /><br />I thought the first 100 pages or so were pretty good. Great, actually. But then it just hits a wall and never quite recovers. Nothing happens for so long that when anything happens, ANYTHING - it doesn't matter how mundane it actually is - it almost feels like picking up a pulse from a week-old corpse before the damn thing croaks again. <br /><br />There were so many problems that I don't even know where to start - flat characterization, half-baked post-apocalyptic elements, poor/uneven pacing, a complete lack of forward momentum... I can go on. I just don't understand how you can come up with an idea this promising... then decide on exploring the least interesting aspects of it. <br /><br />I think Joe Hill is a talented writer, but I just didn't dig this one at all. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/745921-nos4a2-was-my-first-hill-and-i-hated-it">NOS4A2 was my first Hill and I hated it (although I had really high expectations).
I think I&#39;m willing to give him a second chance because the premise of this book looks great, but was wondering if they are similar? - not in story obviously but stylistically I guess?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_745921'><a id="like_count_community_question_745921" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/745921?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">3 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F29875363-the-fireman">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/745921-nos4a2-was-my-first-hill-and-i-hated-it">4 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/745921-nos4a2-was-my-first-hill-and-i-hated-it">See all 12 answers</a>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/7887980-standback"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1379246310i/7887980.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/7887980-standback">Standback</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I found NOS4A2 very different, stylistically, from other Joe Hill works I know and love. I didn't enjoy NOS4A2 either (although I know lots and lots o<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I found NOS4A2 very different, stylistically, from other Joe Hill works I know and love. I didn't enjoy NOS4A2 either (although I know lots and lots of people did!). I felt like it was a book that centered around the generic horror of a couple of shlocky, themed villains; if that was your sense as well, I'd say that others of Hill's works are far, far stronger and more interesting.<br /><br />"The Fireman" isn't out quite yet, so no opinion there yet. But Hill has *lots* of very excellent work out:<br /><br />"Horns" is a fantastic novel with great voice and characters, that tackles its central premise from several different angles.<br />If you're amenable to graphic novels, "Locke and Key" is an absolute masterpiece - the characters are heartbreaking and achingly real; and at the same time it's a story full of wonder and magic (and horror, oh yes.)<br />And his short fiction collection, "20th Century Ghosts," is excellent - with a huge range of stories, each one excellent in a different way.<br /><br />(I found Hill through his novella "Gunpowder," which is a *phenomenal* piece about children with world-shaping abilities, who turn a planet into, well, a powder keg. This novella is currently, unfortunately, *criminally* unavailable anywhere.)<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1567110060" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1567110060" />
    <a title="Will Byrnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes"><img alt="Will Byrnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291462190p2/1526851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1567110060?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Byrnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Byrnes" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes">Will Byrnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780062200631" href="/book/show/25816688-the-fireman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=books-of-the-year-2016">books-of-the-year-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1567110060" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8642379294792850101"><blockquote> <b>The people in charge can always justify doing terrible things in the name of the greater good. A slaughter here, a little torture there. It becomes moral to do things that would be immoral if an ordinary individual did ‘em.</b>
</blockquote>They have been trying to take us down for quite a while. Some may enjoy the end of the world (EotW) arriving in the form of an incoming asteroid. Hey, it worked for the dinosaurs. Alien invasion is always popular. Very big in the 50s, whether by maleficent alien civilization</span>
  <span id="freeText8642379294792850101" style="display:none"><blockquote> <b>The people in charge can always justify doing terrible things in the name of the greater good. A slaughter here, a little torture there. It becomes moral to do things that would be immoral if an ordinary individual did ‘em.</b>
</blockquote>They have been trying to take us down for quite a while. Some may enjoy the end of the world (EotW) arriving in the form of an incoming asteroid. Hey, it worked for the dinosaurs. Alien invasion is always popular. Very big in the 50s, whether by maleficent alien civilizations or maybe a nice juicy mobile plant of the triffid variety. Viruses have been pretty big the last few decades, global pandemics, whether of alien or Terran origin. Zombie apocalypse is all the rage today, whether the zombies are animated by a force of nature or not. How an author takes us from the pre-disaster here through the horrors to there, wherever or whenever there may be, is the fun. But many of these entertainments carry a stowaway. EotW tales exist not only to titillate, and elevate our blood pressure, but to deliver a core of perspective along with the fun. The collapse of civilization is a favorite mechanism for writers looking at the core of human nature. Imaginative tales go to extremes to point out things about the here and now. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1458887558i/18543502._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Joe Hill</b> - from USA Today <br><br>Joe Hill has come up with a truly ingenious mechanism for bringing about <i>his</i> apocalyptic vision. <i>Draco incendia trychophyton</i> is a spore with some unusual properties. (smoothly rolling off the tongue not being one of them) The skin of people who have been exposed to it erupts in what looks like burger meat that has been way, way overcooked, interspersed with lines of gold. The affliction comes to be called “Dragonscale,” or ‘scale for short. It gets worse. Not long after initial exposure, most of the afflicted spontaneously combust. The spore spreads like wildfire, and soon the entire world is ablaze. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1458887558i/18543503.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>This Freightliner snow plow could be better called a <i>Frightliner</i> for its use here</b> <br><br>Harper Grayson, a school nurse, is practically perfect in every way. She is both kind and firm with her young patients, an admirable combination she employs when dealing with adults as well. Her hero is, of course, Mary Poppins. Throughout the 747 pages of this book (the page count may vary with the edition), there are many references to P.L. Travers’ magical nanny, too many to list here. But you should know that Harper totes her belongings in a carpet bag, once had a dog named Bert, and in the imagined film of the story of her life, she wants to be played by Julie Andrews. In an interview for his last book, Hill said<blockquote>
<b> I was thinking about Lon Chaney who had line about, “There’s nothing funny about a clown at midnight.” I think that’s part of the horror writer’s job: to create unsettling juxtapositions. You find something that seems harmless and innocent, and pair it with aspects that are disturbing. Christmas is a joyous occasion, it’s a time of pleasure and family, but there’s something about Christmas songs in the middle of the summer that’s not quite right.</b>- from Nightmare Magazine interview</blockquote>The juxtaposition of Harper’s Disney-ish aspect, which stops just short of animated bluebirds chirping away on her shoulders, adds a nice dollop of sweet to the sour of the apocalyptic landscape. <br><br>Harper and her husband, Jakob, have talked about ending their lives themselves rather than burning to death like the Dragonscale sufferers. But when she discovers that she is pregnant, the appearance of tell-tale black-and-gold on her skin presents not a death sentence, but a challenge. She has seen ‘scaled mothers deliver uninfected babies, and hopes she can too. Jakob has other plans. <br><br>The Fireman of the title is John Rookwood. Harper first encounters him when he insists on crashing the very long line outside the hospital where she is working, (after the school has been shut down) carrying a boy with a severe illness. Later, as vigilante groups spring up to exterminate the infected, so-called Cremation Squads, he leads her to a place of safety. John’s talents extend beyond being handy with a halligan, being kind and protective toward children, and looking steamy in a yellow slicker. He can control his dragonscale, and do some pretty interesting things with it. You wouldn’t want to make his blood boil. John still carries a torch, though, for his old flame. <br><br>	<img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1458886490i/18543423.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>It may not be a roque mallet, but John’s halligan comes in pretty handy</b><br><br><i>The Fireman</i> can be read on at least two levels. On the surface, this is a can’t-put-it-down amazing scifi/horror adventure, a barn-burner of a read, exciting, fun, and very, very scary. It will keep you flipping the pages so fast you might generate sparks. (I recommend reading with a glass or bottle of non-alcoholic liquid near to hand) Harper is a wonderful character. I mean, really, a young nurse, pregnant, fleeing dark forces, while trying to figure out how dragonscale works and how it might be controlled, a woman who is the epitome of cheerful and positive, in a very bleak time, just hoping to live long enough to deliver her baby into the hands of people who can care for him or her. Add a damaged hero in John, an ally who can help her find a haven, if one really exists, but who comes with a bucket brigade of baggage. Good guys, bad guys and plenty in-between, a lot of action and a wealth of creativity. <br><br>All that said, there is something more going on here. This is not just some cozy catastrophe in which a group of survivors carve out a manageable <i>modus vivendi</i> in the shadow of global horror. Hill is not only looking to give his readers a good scare. He wants to offer something more substantive. <i>The Fireman</i> delivers what the best speculative fiction provides, a look at contemporary reality through the lens of fantasy. <br><br>John brings Harper to a place where others with ‘scale have come together, for group support and defense. The place is called Camp Wyndham. And for those to whom the name is unfamiliar, it might help to know that John Wyndham was the author of a 1957 novel, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Midwich_Cuckoos" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Midwich Cuckoos</a>. You might know it better as the film <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Village_of_the_Damned_(1960_film)" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Village of the Damned</a>. The lovely kids in this tale, of dubious parentage, are possessed of a group consciousness. Members of the Camp Wyndham community, all with ‘scale, have found that under certain circumstances the spore allows them to join into a joyous group rapture they call The Bright. This entails a loss of self, which not everyone is all that thrilled about, somewhere between the ecstatic experience of a full-bore revival meeting and a hive mind. Hill also references Jack Finney’s book, made into multiple film versions, <i>The Invasion of the Body Snatchers</i><blockquote>
  <b>Carol said, “Sometimes when I’m in The Bright, I would swear I feel my [late] sister standing right next to me, close enough so I could lay my head on her shoulder, like I used to. When we shine, they all come back to us, you know. The light we make together shows everything that was ever lost to the darkness.<br>Harper clamped down on a shudder. When they spoke of The Bright, they had all the uncomplicated happiness of pod people.</b>
</blockquote> When we lose ourselves in a group-think situation, morality goes out the window. Why have a head at all if you only use it to ditto someone else’s psychotic rage? The relevance to our world is blazingly clear, whether the group be political or religious. There be dragons there. And there is a very real question of whether cooler heads will prevail.<br><br>There is consideration as well of how people reach out to help those in pain or in danger. Maybe like the way Chris Christie locked up a nurse returning to the USA after she had been helping Ebola victims in Africa. In addition to seeing the ‘scalers as infected, see them as unwanted immigrants, as a despised class. See them as Syrian refugees fleeing civil war. See them as Costa Rican children fleeing north to keep from being forcibly drafted into drug gangs. Even a Trump-sized wall cannot keep out a global pandemic. Fear can usually be counted on to drown out most kinder impulses, often with the assistance of small arms. We get a taste of this here, as Cremation Squads do for spore victims what the SS did for Jews and others, or what, I am sure, many Tea-Baggers and militia groups would love to do to progressives, immigrants, members of the LGBT community, and ethnic and religious minorities here in the USA. Erstwhile residents of Auschwitz might recognize how some of the constabulary treat their prisoners in Hill’s dark landscape. All the nastiness is done with the eager support and encouragement, even participation, of a particularly sociopathic hate radio jock, broadcasting on a radio station with appropriate call letters. <br><br>Hill tips his hat to luminary writers who have written about despotism and apocalypse. In addition to John Wyndham, noted above, a boat is named for Margaret Atwood, author of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Handmaid’s Tale</a> and the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/91437810" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">MaddAddam</a> series. Nobel laureate Alexander Solzhenitsyn, author of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Gulag_Archipelago" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Gulag Archipelago</a> gets a mention, as does Cormac McCarthy, for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/34559379" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Road</a>. There are almost certainly more of these.<br><br>Over his entire oeuvre, Hill has demonstrated considerable glee in referencing the popular culture in which he was raised. MTV VJ Martha Quinn gets a lot of ink. Song, TV and movie references abound. But his largest source of material here is Disney. The Mary Poppins references in support of Harper’s character are legion. But there are plenty of others, including <i>Toy Story</i>, and a somewhat more oblique reference to Pinocchio. A ref to a classic Coke commercial also resonates, creepily. He even references his own work a bit. I spotted one link to <i>NOS4A2</i>, but I bet there are plenty more. I did not check the DNA of character names against the Stephen-King-Joe-Hill-character-database (there probably is one) of names used in their books, but generally, Joe has taken to tossing in refs to his dad’s work. These are always fun to spot. The writing of one particular character here is very reminiscent of Jack Torrance’s magnum opus in <i>The Shining</i>. Not so much the form, but the impact, and the revelation it contains about that character. As with the haunted Torrance, this guy blames others for all his problems. And <i>shining</i> is referenced as well, although of a sort different from that possessed by Danny at the Overlook, when the Camp Wyndham folks link up in The Bright. Hill has even said of <i>The Fireman</i> “it’s my version of <i>The Stand</i>, soaked in gasoline and set on fire.” <br><br>So what comes next? Fire is often used as a cleansing image, in nature and religion. Burning the earth, as Maine, and the world, is scorched, may allow new growth, in the same way that new growth arrived in the years after Mount St Helen’s blew. Is that a factor here? Cleaning via fire so something new can grow? I won’t burn the ending for you, but it did suggest that Hill will be adding some logs to the flames of this story in future volumes. No inside intel, just a guess. <br><br>I am tempted to suggest that readers of <i>The Fireman</i> will feel the burn, but that might imply that Hill has indicated a preference in the Democratic primary for Bernie Sanders. <a href="https://medium.com/@joe_hill/not-over-the-hill-a77fc7a61cbc#.fxww3vcd2" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">He has not.</a> But I <i>can</i> say that <i>The Fireman</i> is certain to be both one of the hottest books of the year, and one of the coolest.<br><br><br>Review posted March 25, 2016<br><br>Publication date – May 7, 2016<br><br><br>=============================<b>EXTRA STUFF</b><br><br>In the epigraph to the novel. Hill includes three diverse quotes, one from Springsteen, one from <i>Mary Poppins</i>, and one from <i>Fahrenheit 451</i>. All were sources of inspiration. It should come as no surprise that before settling on the final title, the original name for Bradbury’s classic was “The Fireman.”<br><br>Links to the author’s <a href="https://www.joehillfiction.com/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">personal</a>, <a href="https://www.instagram.com/joe_hill/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> and <a href="http://joehillsthrills.tumblr.com/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Tumblr</a> pages<br><br>You can read <a href="http://www.ew.com/article/2015/12/09/joe-hill-fireman-cover-excerpt" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">an excerpt</a> from the book on Entertainment Weekly<br><br>Interviews<br>----- <a href="http://www.buzzfeed.com/hayleycampbell/joe-hill-calls-bullshit#.unW6LmjJX%20" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"> Joe Hill Calls Bullshit On The Crazy Artist Cliché</a> on Buzzfeed – by Haley Campbell<br>-----<a href="http://www.writersbone.com/interviewsarchive/2015/7/6/pouring-gasoline-on-the-fire-with-horror-author-joe-hill" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"> Pouring Gasoline On the Fire With Horror Author Joe Hill</a> on Writers Bone – by Sean Tuohy<br>-----<a href="http://www.nightmare-magazine.com/nonfiction/interview-joe-hill-part-1/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Interview: Joe Hill (Part 1)</a> by The Geeks Guide to the Galaxy for Nightmare: Horror and Dark Fantasy<br><br>Reviews of other Joe Hill Books<br>-----<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2923249839" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Full Throttle</a><br>-----<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2058840059" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Strange Weather</a><br>-----<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/483336047" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">NOS4A2</a> <br>-----<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/39354251" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">20th Century Ghosts</a><br>-----<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/35744748" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Heart-Shaped Box</a> <br><br>December 6, 2016 - <i>The Fireman</i> is voted the GoodReads Choice award winner for Best Horror book of 2016</span>
  <a data-text-id="8642379294792850101" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1522850627?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780062200631" href="/book/show/25816688-the-fireman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=germs-viruses">germs-viruses</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=spooky-powers">spooky-powers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=apocalypse-now">apocalypse-now</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=damn-dirty-hippies">damn-dirty-hippies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1522850627" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9596339998717232549">Well, that was a spoonful of nonsense.<br/><br/>It had a promising start with the outbreak of a disease known as Dragonscale which first manifests as dark marks on the skin. Getting a free tribal tattoo might not sound that bad, but the real problem is that eventually infected people burst into flames and burn to death. The damage caused by walking blowtorches and the fear of being infected have society teetering on the brink of collapse.<br/><br/>Harper is a young nurse who discovers that she has contracted Dragon</span>
  <span id="freeText9596339998717232549" style="display:none">Well, that was a spoonful of nonsense.<br /><br />It had a promising start with the outbreak of a disease known as Dragonscale which first manifests as dark marks on the skin. Getting a free tribal tattoo might not sound that bad, but the real problem is that eventually infected people burst into flames and burn to death. The damage caused by walking blowtorches and the fear of being infected have society teetering on the brink of collapse.<br /><br />Harper is a young nurse who discovers that she has contracted Dragonscale and she’s pregnant. If that isn’t bad enough her jerk-face husband Jakob goes coocoo for Coco-Puffs and thinks they should just kill themselves. During a desperate moment Harper finds help in the form of a mysterious guy dressed as a fireman who leads her to a hidden community of infected people who have found a way to survive the disease. Unfortunately, discord within that group proves as dangerous as the vigilante Cremation Squads that have started murdering the infected.<br /><br />It’s a strong premise, but unfortunately there’s a number of factors that drag it down. First and foremost is that it’s way too long. Hill can’t seem to commit to one main story, and he keeps adding on to it like a late-night TV commercial promising, “But that’s not all!” This causes a lot of drift with a long swath of the book not even touching on what’s going on in the outside world and forgetting what should be major characters for long periods of time. It’s also like one of those action movies that never seems to know when to end that goes on 20 minutes past the point where it should have wrapped things up.<br /><br />I also wasn’t a fan of Harper, and since this whole story is built on the idea of a plucky heroine trying to survive a civilization ending plague then I needed to have at least have some respect for her. Unfortunately, she comes across as twit who never seems to wise up until something terrible happens. Which it does. Repeatedly. I lost count of the number of times where she is shocked by the bad intentions of someone and says things like, “You can’t!” It’s the apocalype, lady. They can, and they will. Her infatuation with Mary Poppins, and Hill’s constant use of it and its songs are also way overdone. <br /><br />In fact, there’s just too much goddamn music in this book overall with constant quoting of lyrics and talking about various musicians. It's a crutch Hill leans on far too often. Plus, it’s all Jurassic Rock with a smattering of ‘80s pop in there with even an old VJ from MTV having a role to play. It’s 2016, Joe Hill. I don’t need your main character, who is supposedly in her early twenties, lecturing me on what the preference for the Rolling Stones or the Beatles says about a person. <br /><br />Another piece that flies off this jalopy of a book once it gets up to speed is the nature of the disease itself. There’s a lot of effort spent to convince us that there is a rational scientific reason that people would turn into Zippos, and I can suspend disbelief enough to go with that concept. But when more and more is added to the point where we’re into ideas like people being able to generate and control fire without their clothes burning and even more weirdness then you don’t need Neal deGrasse Tyson to call bullshit on it. Just as he couldn’t seem to commit to one story or another Hill can’t seem to decide if he wanted a more grounded concept with some science behind it or if he wanted to jump full-on into the supernatural pool.<br /><br />Hill also opted to run home to Daddy in this because the entire book is absolutely rotten with Easter eggs of Stephen King’s work. A few references can be fun, but when Hill essentially ‘borrows’ a character from <i>The Stand</i> including a cute little name trick to underline it then it’s crossed the line. (Harold Cross? For a character who is essentially Harold Lauder? That's weak.*) After a while it started to seem desperate, as if Hill knew things weren’t going well and hoped he might use fan familiarity of his father's books to invoke some of his magic. Hill also seems to have inherited his father’s trait of having a bunch of characters claim that they’re are critically short of time only to have them waste most of it with idle chit-chat and banter that is supposed to be funny and make you like the characters. It’s not, and it doesn’t.<br /><br />So at this point Hill is 2 for 4 with me, and after this I’m going to need a really good reason to pick up his next one.<br /><br /><i>* And I didn't think about this until I read Edward Lorn's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1257314457?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">review</a> where he pointed out that there's also a deaf character named Nick. Come on, Joe Hill. You're better than that.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="9596339998717232549" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1053760-shelby-trains-flying-monkeys?shelf=edelweiss">edelweiss</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1420193744" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12043759766232186480"><b>Did you read about the bacterium that thrives in volcanoes, right on the edge of boiling rock? That's us. Humanity is a germ that thrives on the very edge of catastrophe.</b><br/><br/>It shows up on your skin looking like a form of stripes. Beautiful black and gold markings. Dragonscale aka <i>Draco Incendia Trychophyton</i> is a contagion in which the person will end up being burned alive. There is no cure.<br/><a href='http://www.freeimagehosting.net/commercial-photography/california/palm-springs/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1454188182ra/17929380.gif' alt='Palm Springs commercial photography' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/>Harper is a nurse who has been working in a hospital day and night as a volunteer, trying to help anyone w</span>
  <span id="freeText12043759766232186480" style="display:none"><b>Did you read about the bacterium that thrives in volcanoes, right on the edge of boiling rock? That's us. Humanity is a germ that thrives on the very edge of catastrophe.</b><br><br>It shows up on your skin looking like a form of stripes. Beautiful black and gold markings. Dragonscale aka <i>Draco Incendia Trychophyton</i> is a contagion in which the person will end up being burned alive. There is no cure.<br><a href="http://www.freeimagehosting.net/commercial-photography/california/palm-springs/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1454188182ra/17929380.gif" alt="Palm Springs commercial photography" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Harper is a nurse who has been working in a hospital day and night as a volunteer, trying to help anyone with the Dragonscale. One night a fireman brings in a young deaf boy, both of them are infected but the boy later disappears. Harper had stepped forward to help the two of them and the fireman told her that if she ever needed help with a fire that he hoped to help her.<br><br>The hospital that Harper is working at later burns. Everything is burning. Too many people combusting and it can't be stopped. She goes home to her wanna-be author husband. They get the freaky deaky going and Harper becomes infected and then realizes she is also pregnant. (This isn't really spoilery) Jakob, her husband wants to just go ahead and end things before Harper burns and is convinced that he is also infected with Dragonscale.<br>Harper does have a spine despite her Mary Poppins infatuation and tells him to screw off.<br> <a href="http://www.freeimagehosting.net/commercial-photography/california/palm-springs/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1454188182ra/17929381.gif" alt="Palm Springs commercial photography" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Jakob tries to help out Harper with her problems anyways. Jakob is an asshole.<br>Harper is alone, pregnant and has a psycho trying to kill her and her unborn child. Enter in The Fireman.<br><a href="http://www.freeimagehosting.net/commercial-photography/california/palm-springs/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1454188182ra/17929382.gif" alt="Palm Springs commercial photography" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>whew..it just got hot up in this review.....<br><br>The fireman takes Harper to a camp where people infected with Dragonscale have learned a few tricks to dealing with the condition. They aren't burning. They are flourishing. The fireman is a totally different thing/person/who the hell knows all to himself.<br><br>I'm going to admit. I think this book could have lost some pages, because it is a big old chunk, it does tend to run on in some spots. Then in some spots I was glued to the pages and lost sleep. All through the book though I was entertained. Hill told a story and I thought he did a damn fine job of it.<br>I LOVE this type of story and I've seen people comparing it to Joe Hill's dad's book "The Stand"...I just didn't see that. I saw a story all in it's own. Why does everyone continue to insist on comparing the two authors? I think Joe Hill has earned his frigging stripes. Stop it.<br>There were some touches that reminded me of stories I've heard before, but I ate it all up like candy. It didn't seem like he was trying to copy dear old dad. <br><a href="http://www.freeimagehosting.net/commercial-photography/california/palm-springs/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1454188182ra/17929383.gif" alt="Palm Springs commercial photography" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>There is a bit of humor mixed in with the terror. Little tidbits of other books I've loved. (Joe Hill is a total Harry Potter fan) and I think I have to take back my early hate of Joe Hill. (I didn't like anything he wrote up until <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15729539.NOS4A2" title="NOS4A2 by Joe Hill" rel="nofollow noopener">NOS4A2</a>)<br><br><b>"Do you think Keith Richards is still alive?" he asked.<br>"Sure. Nothing can kill him. He'll outlast us all."</b><br><a href="http://www.freeimagehosting.net/commercial-photography/california/palm-springs/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1454188182ra/17929384.gif" alt="Palm Springs commercial photography" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><i>Booksource: Edelweiss in exchange for review.</i><br><br><a href="http://www.freeimagehosting.net/commercial-photography/california/palm-springs/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454188182i/17929385.jpg" alt="Palm Springs commercial photography" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Not many reviews are up for this book at the time of my posting. I couldn't wait once I got my greedy hands on this one and neither could <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1517197527?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Mallory Hearts Reviews</a> because we know what is the good stuff. :D</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1720620-anne?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>,

<span id="freeTextContainer7046839665260378223">2.5 stars<br/><br/><i>Ehhhhhhh.</i><br/><b>Boy, that ending petered out like a squeaky fart, didn't it?</b><br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1473472761ra/20469462.gif' width='400' height='300' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I mean, the beginning was good. I got sucked in by the whole Dragonscale/apocalypse thing. The middle was good<i>ish</i>, but it meandered around too much. Then the last part leading up to the ending had a bit of the nail biter going for it, but the actual <i>ending</i>?<br/>WTF, dude? Where's my payoff for reading this enormous fucker?!<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1473471865ra/20469407.gif' width='400' height='300' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>In the interest of transparency, I should mention that I'm the kind of reader that gets butthurt by en</span>
  <span id="freeText7046839665260378223" style="display:none">2.5 stars<br><br><i>Ehhhhhhh.</i><br><b>Boy, that ending petered out like a squeaky fart, didn't it?</b><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1473472761ra/20469462.gif" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I mean, the beginning was good. I got sucked in by the whole Dragonscale/apocalypse thing. The middle was good<i>ish</i>, but it meandered around too much. Then the last part leading up to the ending had a bit of the nail biter going for it, but the actual <i>ending</i>?<br>WTF, dude? Where's my payoff for reading this enormous fucker?!<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1473471865ra/20469407.gif" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>In the interest of transparency, I should mention that I'm the kind of reader that gets butthurt by endings I don't understand, like, or agree with. <br>So. There's <i>that</i>.<br><br>Ok, for everyone who loved this book, I hope you don't think I'm belittling Hill, or your taste. I really loved <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/317384994" rel="nofollow noopener"> Locke &amp; Key</a>, but this is the only other thing I've read of his, and (while I think he's probably a good writer) I'm not really sure what the hell just happened here. For such a promising start, I really felt like he shit the bed by the last page.<br>Ok. Maybe that's not a fair assessment of this book, but I <i>did</i> feel disappointed when it was over. <br>So. Yeah. I didn't get the hype. At. All.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1473474580ra/20469592.gif" width="400" height="250" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Also, fuck all the Mary Poppins references. It's not <i>that</i> awesome of a movie, and I have very little respect for a grown woman who imagines herself to be a budding Ms. Poppins.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1473474580ra/20469593.gif" width="400" height="250" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br> <br>In the end, I'd say it's just not <i>my</i> type of book. It's not horrible, but I can't see myself recommending it, either.<br><br><i>Psst...if you're hoping for <b>this</b> kind of Fireman, you're going to be sorely disappointed.<br>Not that <b>I</b> thought this was about a sexy fireman. Nope.<br>*cough*<br>But, seriously. I wouldn't have complained.</i><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1473472480ra/20469439.gif" width="400" height="350" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1257314457" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1257314457" />
    <a title="Edward Lorn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn"><img alt="Edward Lorn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492199506p2/8850418.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1257314457?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward Lorn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward Lorn" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn">Edward Lorn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780062200631" href="/book/show/25816688-the-fireman">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">No one</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1257314457" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6565871397635487037"><b>WINNER OF BIGGEST DISAPPOINTMENT OF 2016!<br/><br/>UPDATE: Dropping this to two stars because it really was only an "okay" read. In time it might drop to a one star, because the more I think about this book, the more I dislike it.</b><br/><br/>It is with a heavy heart that I must give this book <s>three</s> two stars. Last night, I was certain it would be four stars (this was before I realized how short-sighted the novel really is), but after much consideration, I just couldn't do it. Hopefully, I can effectively explain why.</span>
  <span id="freeText6565871397635487037" style="display:none"><b>WINNER OF BIGGEST DISAPPOINTMENT OF 2016!<br /><br />UPDATE: Dropping this to two stars because it really was only an "okay" read. In time it might drop to a one star, because the more I think about this book, the more I dislike it.</b><br /><br />It is with a heavy heart that I must give this book <s>three</s> two stars. Last night, I was certain it would be four stars (this was before I realized how short-sighted the novel really is), but after much consideration, I just couldn't do it. Hopefully, I can effectively explain why.<br /><br />Before I ever started this book, I said to myself, "Self, you're not going to mention Stephen King or <i>The Stand</i> in your review. Hill has proven himself as his own author, and he gained an audience before anyone knew he was King's son. So, yeah, don't mention that shit because to do so would make you an asshole. Cool? Cool." <br /><br />Well fuck me. I can't do that. Yes, <i>The Fireman</i> is Joe Hill's book, but it is so packed full of Stephen King Easter eggs and nods to <i>The Stand</i> that it is impossible for me <b>not</b> to talk about them. So I will get them out of the way up front. Damn near every chapter of this book has a reference to Stephen King. The book is nothing like King's post-apocalyptic masterpiece, but it is stuffed to the brim with plot points and characters that mirror King's novel. But this is not to say that you're missing something if you haven't read <i>The Stand</i> (well, you're missing a lot, considering it's a fantastic book, but that's not the point I'm trying to make). Let's say you haven't read <i>The Stand</i> (shame on you) and you decide to pick up <i>The Fireman</i>. If you do so, you'll have a complete, thrilling experience with no confusion, because the Easter eggs are hidden in the way Easter eggs should be hidden. In such a way as to make fans giddy and those not in the know not feel confused. For instance: there's a character named Nick in this book. Nick is a deaf kid. Nick Andros from <i>The Stand</i> was also deaf. If you haven't read King's book, you're not missing anything. Every single Easter egg in this book is like that. Unlike some authors (myself included), Hill never makes you feel as if you're missing something. I dig that very much. I had a fucking blast finding all the references, and my only complaint about the amount of Easter eggs is that Hill only dropped one for his own work. I would have loved to see something about Judas Coyne or Charlie Manx or the murder of a girl named Merrin or a couple people discussing an inflatable boy or a nod to a horror story about the Buttonboy or a comic titled Locke &amp; Key. Hill's brand is full of fantastic lore and characters, and I feel as if he doesn't value his own work as much as he values his father's. Hill spent so much time and effort distancing himself from his father's legacy so he could rise on the current of his own abilities, but now that everyone knows his real name is Joseph Hillstrom King, it feels like he's playing second fiddle to Pops. That being said, this book shines with Hill's love for his old man. I get it. I simply hope that, in the future, there's more Hill in Hill's work and less King. <br /><br />Wow, I went off on a tangent. Back to the story.<br /><br /><i>The Fireman</i> is an odd bird; a mixture of the beginning and middle of <i>The Stand</i> and Rowling's <i>Harry Potter and the Order of the Phoenix</i>. The former more so than the latter. That's right: this book is more like Harry Potter than it is <i>The Stand</i>. Most of my reasoning will be in the Spoiler Discussion at the end of this post, but suffice it to say, the book is built on the same chassis. Hill even mentions Rowling in his dedication, thanking her for showing him how to tell his story. In my opinion, though, Hill tells the better story. But, there are certain sections that outright steal from Rowling's book. Some might call these tributes, but because Hill's story strongly depends on certain plot points he borrowed from Rowling, I call it stealing. Once again, more on that in the Spoiler Discussion section. Because there is so much in this book Hill got from other sources, I cannot give the book a perfect score. I didn't imagine I would ever say this about a Joe Hill book, but the amount of unoriginal ideas in this novel is staggering. The more I think about it, the more it feels like <i>Armada</i>, by Ernest Cline, only better written and not so obnoxiously obvious with its pop culture referencing. Hill's tributes are subtle, but there are so many of them they start to form a pile you cannot ignore. <br /><br />That being said, <i>The Fireman</i> is highly readable. One of the most well written books I've read this year. His prose sings, its so well tuned. Even the lulls engage the reader, and I never felt as if I was reading information I could do without. <br /><br />A note on the hardcover version: It's fucking gorgeous. There's almost imperceptible dots of ash on every page, but they never interfere with the readability of the words. I dig the cover, too, which is a perfect blend of gloss and matte finish. The attention to detail is stunning. So nice.<br /><br />And everything else I want to talk about is spoiler related, so if you've read this one, I'll see you in the Spoiler Discussion.<br /><br />In summation: While unoriginal to the point it becomes predictable, the book is an <s>fun</s> okay time. I want to see Hill go back to his own brand next time, though, instead of playing along the path of the beam. I hope that he'll continue to build on King's lore instead of stealing from it. There's nothing wrong with a tribute as long as it's transformative in nature. Anything less is stealing. And that's what this felt like; a friendly robbery. <br /><br /><b>Final Judgment: Not entirely Hill's creation.</b><br /><br />Spoiler Discussion: Wherein I discuss and spoil <i>The Fireman</i>, <i>The Stand</i>, and <i>Harry Potter and the Order of the Phoenix</i>.<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br /><br />Yo! How's it going? Great book, huh? I enjoyed it, anyways. So, how much referencing did Hill do? Lemme count the ways:<br /><br />1. Deaf character named Nick is obvious reference to Nick Andros from <i>The Stand</i>. See also: Asshole/traitor character named Harold Cross/Harold Lauder of <i>The Stand</i> and the pregnant Harper Francis "Frannie" Grayson/the pregnant Francis "Frannie" Goldsmith from <i>The Stand</i>. <br /><br />2. Twice, Hill writes that a character "forgot the face of their father", which is a reference to King's Dark Tower series.<br /><br />3. Hill describes those with Drangonscale as "shining", which could be a loose reference to his father's book <i>The Shining</i>. Also, in the dedication to <i>The Shining</i>, King says, "This is for Joe Hill King, who shines on." In <i>The Fireman</i>, Hill says in his dedication, "For Ethan John King, who burns bright."<br /><br />4. A character named Mindy shouts "My life for...", which is a reference to Trashcan Man in <i>The Stand</i> who was wont to say, "My life for you!" Although Mindy does not say "you", she uses actual character names.<br /><br />5. There's a face in the fire ala Sirius Black in the Harry Potter books. Other Potter plot points are: female dictator-type character who is unbending and ruthless; much teen angst from Allie; a secret society that might as well be called the Order of the Phoenix because The Fireman creates an actual fucking phoenix with his powers; a wizard (in this case a burner) coming into their own and learning how to use their powers; and so on. This book really is <i>Harry Potter and the Order of the Phoenix</i> just with a brand new paint job. Sad part is, I never would have noticed if Hill hadn't said, "J.K. Rowling, whose stories showed me how to write this one" in the dedication page. <br /><br />6. A reference to Tom Gordon from <i>The Girl Who Loved Tom Gordon</i>. I know Tom Gordon is/was a real baseball player, but think about all the baseball players in the world. Don't you find it funny that Hill chose to use him of all people?<br /><br />7. Nozz-a-la soda is a nod to King's Dark Tower series, which puts Hill's book on the path of the beam.<br /><br />9. Flaming hand of God saves the day. I don't care if in Hill's book Nick makes the hand, this is an obvious nod to how King ends <i>The Stand</i>.<br /><br />8. John Rookwood's fire truck falls into a collapsed overpass much like Stu Redman of <i>The Stand</i> falls into one in King's book. Both men break their leg and are unable to go forward of their own power. John does continue the journey, but must be dragged, whereas Stu is left behind. This entire ending was also reminiscent of Roland's battle with infection in <i>The Drawing of the Three</i>. <br /><br />9. CHRISTMASLAND!!! Finally, Hill references his own work!<br /><br />To wrap up, if you guys didn't read the credits at the end of the book, you really, really should. The story continues there, where we find out the real ending. <br /><br />This has been fun. Thanks for joining me.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="6565871397635487037" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1847587960" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1847587960" />
    <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten"><img alt="Jeffrey Keeten" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301087352p2/3427339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1847587960?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Keeten" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780062200631" href="/book/show/25816688-the-fireman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1847587960" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6105120591187458505"><b>”...Her left arm was sheet music. Delicate black lines spooled around and around her forearm, bars as thin as the strands of a spiderweb, with what looked like golden notes scattered across them. She found herself pulling her sleeve back to look at it every few minutes. By the end of the following week, she was sketched in Dragonscale from wrist to shoulder. <br/><br/>When she got over feeling winded and sick, she had to admit to herself that it was curiously beautiful.”</b><br/><br/><a href='https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Fireman_zpsa7fkaq6v.jpg.html' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536846676i/26296927._SY540_.jpg' alt=' photo Fireman_zpsa7fkaq6v.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/>When Harper Grayson comes down </span>
  <span id="freeText6105120591187458505" style="display:none"><b>”...Her left arm was sheet music. Delicate black lines spooled around and around her forearm, bars as thin as the strands of a spiderweb, with what looked like golden notes scattered across them. She found herself pulling her sleeve back to look at it every few minutes. By the end of the following week, she was sketched in Dragonscale from wrist to shoulder. <br><br>When she got over feeling winded and sick, she had to admit to herself that it was curiously beautiful.”</b><br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Fireman_zpsa7fkaq6v.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536846676i/26296927._SY540_.jpg" alt=" photo Fireman_zpsa7fkaq6v.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>When Harper Grayson comes down with Dragonscale, A.K.A. Draco Incendia Trychophyton, it is a death sentence. The contagion spreads quickly through the body, wrapping its tendrils around the skin, leaving behind these beautiful shimmering scales that eventually start to smoke until they reach a certain maturity or the person comes under a large amount of stress. They then spontaneously combust, experience cellular combustion, develop contagion points, and disease vectors are produced. We can be as scientific as we want to be, but the end result is the infected turn to ash. <br><br><b>This disease is highly contagious. </b><br><br>When one person lights up, it can cause a chain reaction where anyone with dragonscale in the area joins the inferno. <br><br>When the crisis hit, Harper volunteers at the local hospital wanting to help anyway she can. She is careful, wears a hazmat suit, but the problem is there is a lot of conflicting information on how the contagion is spread. Almost simultaneously with learning she has dragonscale, she discovers she is pregnant. <br><br>Joy crushed by the certainty of death. The disease moves so fast in most people that it wouldn’t be the right time to start reading <i>War &amp; Peace</i> or <i>Infinite Jest</i>. You might want to think more in terms of <i>The Bridge Over San Luis Rey</i>.<br><br>Her condescending, but loving, husband Jakob is furious. He becomes fixated on them ending their lives now even though he doesn’t have the contagion. There is much more to be learned about Jakob, so as you read the book, be sure to keep a baseball bat hidden down the back of your leg because you are probably going to need it. <br><br><b>”Harper supposed it did not pay to be too impressed with a man just because he could ride a unicycle.”</b> A good rule of thumb for every woman to keep in mind. :-) <br><br>People are dying so fast in such numbers few are not directly affected. Those who live lose as much or more than those who die. Too many people are gone too quickly. <i>”We are taught to think of personality as a singular, private possession. All the ideas and beliefs and attitudes that make you <b>you</b>--we are raised to believe them to be a set of files stored in the lockbox of the brain. Most people have no idea how much of themselves they store off-site. Your personality is not just a matter of what you know about yourself, but what others know about you. You are one person with your mother, and another with your lover, and yet another with your child. Those other people create you--finish you--as much as you create you. When you’re gone, the ones you’ve left behind get to keep the same part of you they always had.”</i><br><br>You may still have that part of the people you have lost, but it is like having a garden without water or having a flower in the window that the sun never reaches. Without seeing that person, revitalizing that connection, eventually it will wither and die. <br><br>With the help of a man referred to as the Fireman, Harper finds her way to a small community of followers who have figured out how to control the disease. <i>”Cortisol kicks off spontaneous combustion. But oxytocin--the social-networking hormone--puts the Dragonscale at ease.”</i> In other words, stress releases cortisol which inflames the disease, but oxytocin, produced by say singing together, turns the disease into a benign light show.<br><br>I’d be humming the <i>Sound of Music</i> 24/7. <br><br>On the outside of the community is a radio personality who calls himself the Marlboro Man. He has assembled a crew of executioners who travel around looking for the infected so they can eradicate them. There are always those people who are kept in check by society, but once civilization starts to crumble, their true nature emerges, and we discover that what they really like to do is kill people. The fear of the infection is just an excuse for them to drop the pretenses surrounding their own warped and blackened soul and be who they have always wanted to be...a killer. <br><br>Things go well at the community of the infected for a while, but as always seems to happen with human beings, eventually a power struggle breaks out. The camp breaks into factions. The one man who can actually control the dragonfire and use it as a weapon, the Fireman, is the man they all respect/fear. He lives with the community, but on an island away from the rest of them. He has his own demons he is wrestling with, and fire is just one of them. <br><br>As the world goes up in flames and the sanctuary community starts to come apart, the only beacon of hope is Martha Quinn’s voice, a blast from MTV of the 1980s, broadcasting over the radio about a sanctuary on an island off the coast of New England. To get there will become an odyssey as arduous as navigating the terrain from Cormac McCarthy’s <i>The Road</i>. <br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Joe20Hill20Firetruck_zps6w8vnvnl.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536846676i/26296929.jpg" alt=" photo Joe20Hill20Firetruck_zps6w8vnvnl.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>The author Joe Hill</b><br><br>I am a huge fan of post-apocalyptic books. It is a part of me I’m still exploring to determine exactly why I find them uplifting. Is there a twisted part of my psyche that wants the civilised world to end? Am I as crazy as the Cotton Mather followers of the early 18th century? Am I so bored with my existence that I feel a good end of world scenario would shake me out of my doldrums? I don’t think I suffer from any of those things, but self-delusion is always a good possibility. I think what is more likely is that I like seeing humanity when their backs are against the wall, their ass is stuck in the darkest, deepest crack, and absolute annihilation of the human species is on the verge of becoming a reality, and we find a way to survive. <br><br>If you wish to see more of my most recent book and movie reviews, visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.jeffreykeeten.com">http://www.jeffreykeeten.com</a><br>I also have a Facebook blogger page at:<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten">https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="6105120591187458505" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1645025808" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1645025808" />
    <a title="Carol" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7668522-carol"><img alt="Carol" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546364878p2/7668522.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1645025808?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol" href="/user/show/7668522-carol">Carol</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780062200631" href="/book/show/25816688-the-fireman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=read-2016">read-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=published-2016">published-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1645025808" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7095324967443258853"><b>3.5 Stars</b><p>The world was suddenly catching fire........such a promising beginning........such a wonderfully threatening title and book-cover, <b>but........</b></p><p><b>THE FIREMAN</b> just wasn't what I was expecting or at least hoping for. Without giving any part of this "entertaining" story away; yes, it was indeed entertaining....entertaining, but often predictable and a bit ludicrous here and there, but still <b>very</b> entertaining!</p><p>After the excellent beginning, the story travels a different path that I won't divulge</p></span>
  <span id="freeText7095324967443258853" style="display:none"><b>3.5 Stars</b><p>The world was suddenly catching fire........such a promising beginning........such a wonderfully threatening title and book-cover, <b>but........</b></p><p><b>THE FIREMAN</b> just wasn't what I was expecting or at least hoping for. Without giving any part of this "entertaining" story away; yes, it was indeed entertaining....entertaining, but often predictable and a bit ludicrous here and there, but still <b>very</b> entertaining!</p><p>After the excellent beginning, the story travels a different path that I won't divulge here except to say I wanted to experience more of the whole world's post-apocalyptic existence, and I wanted <b>THE FIREMAN</b> himself to be, well, more than he was.......</p><p>Overall, I enjoyed it, snickered at the <i>many</i> King-isms along the way, and really didn't mind the 747 pages, but truth be told, sadly, it just didn't light my fire.</p><p>Thank you Orion Publishing Group and NetGalley for the ARC!</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="7095324967443258853" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1641064057" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1641064057" />
    <a title="Susanne  Strong" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4514828-susanne-strong"><img alt="Susanne  Strong" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583087410p2/4514828.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1641064057?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susanne  Strong" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susanne  Strong" href="/user/show/4514828-susanne-strong">Susanne  Strong</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780062200631" href="/book/show/25816688-the-fireman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4514828-susanne-strong?shelf=favorite-authors">favorite-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4514828-susanne-strong?shelf=must-read">must-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4514828-susanne-strong?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4514828-susanne-strong?shelf=2016-five-star-books">2016-five-star-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1641064057" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15289847684348299204"><b>5 Stars.<br/>An absolutely stellar novel by Joe Hill! Vastly different than all of his other books & superbly well written! I didn't want it to end. </b><br/><br/><blockquote><br/>REVISED: 6.12.17.<br/>I listened to the audiobook version in two days last week (which is now available on Hoopla) even though I've already read the book. I couldn't help myself - the incredible Kate Mulgrew narrated it and she also narrated Joe Hill's NOS4A2. She made that book sound even scarier than it already was - thus listening to The Fireman was kind o</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText15289847684348299204" style="display:none"><b>5 Stars.<br />An absolutely stellar novel by Joe Hill! Vastly different than all of his other books &amp; superbly well written! I didn't want it to end. </b><br /><br /><blockquote><br />REVISED: 6.12.17.<br />I listened to the audiobook version in two days last week (which is now available on Hoopla) even though I've already read the book. I couldn't help myself - the incredible Kate Mulgrew narrated it and she also narrated Joe Hill's NOS4A2. She made that book sound even scarier than it already was - thus listening to The Fireman was kind of irresistible. The audiobook version of The Fireman and "Dragonscale" was much more horrific, <u>more real.</u> When I was listening, I could see it happening all around me. Like in a Science Fiction novel.. one I couldn't run away from and oddly enough, given the premise, didn't want to. </blockquote><br /><br />Draco Incendia Trychophyton, aka, Dragonscale, or the 'Scale. It's kind of like a superbug that everyone catches because it's highly contagious and it's discovered by interesting black and gold markings on one's skin, causing those who are afflicted to burst into flames. It's caused by a spore that floats through the air via ash, and once everyone catches it, they spontaneously combust into flames, The premise is totally wicked. And scary as hell.<br /><br /><b>The Fireman, is in my opinion the most sophisticated novel Joe Hill has written to date. </b><br /><br />The main characters are extremely well developed and well written: Nurse Harper Willowes is a fierce, strong willed, standout heroine. Allie is one heck of an incredibly tough teen who doesn't take any sh&amp;t from anyone. The Fireman, John Rookwood's, ability to control the fire burning from within is truly amazing but all of his human flaws, that is what makes him a man. And Nick - the little boy who lost his mom and shut down against almost everyone - and then in the face of the gravest danger, does what he has to do to take care of the only family he has left. That's powerful stuff! And then there's Jakob, Nurse Willowes, husband. He's becomes obsessed; with trying not to contract Dragonscale, and trying to murder his wife because she is a carrier of the disease and is also pregnant. <br /><br />In my opinion, this novel flows in a way that none of his other books have yet to achieve. <br /><br />And even with that, I can't say that this is my favorite Joe Hill novel, though I liked it a lot. I gave it 5 stars for his writing style being much improved and for the truly interesting idea of a spore that is highly contagious causing people who come into contact with it, to incinerate. It's ingenious and the characters in the storyline are pretty interesting too. Harper, John, Renae, Allie, Nick, Father Storey, Jakob: These people: They are this story. And it is well worth the read!<br /><br />For me, Joe Hill's NOS4A2, was my favorite book of the last few years, no question. Vic McQueen was the best heroine ever. Every part of that book was a wild ride. But not every part was smooth and beautifully well written. NOS4A2 was a bumpy ride. And it was a ride you didn't want to get off. With five stars for the amazing characters, crazy heart stopping moments and lots of tears.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15289847684348299204" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
